Detailed Application of the Three-Year Deferment
Detailed scenarios of diyah collection periods

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to calculate the collection period for diyah based on the amount and determine when stipends are taken from future or past years.

Core Idea
The three-year deferment is a maximum; smaller amounts are collected over fewer years, and if stipends come in a lump sum, the entire diyah is taken at once.

This lesson explores the detailed rules for applying the three-year deferment of diyah, including partial diyah, multiple killers, and the timing of stipends. The passage explains that the goal is alleviation, so the collection pattern adjusts to achieve that goal.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base ruling
The base ruling is that full diyah is collected over three years from the diwan's stipends.
This is the default for full diyah.
2
Examine the partial diyah rule
If the diyah is one-third or less, it is collected in one year; if more than one-third up to two-thirds, in two years; for full diyah, three years.
The collection period is proportional to the amount.
3
Understand the lump-sum rule
If the stipends of three future years come out in one year, the entire diyah is taken at once, because the goal of alleviation is already achieved.
Past stipends (before judgment) are not used.
4
Apply to multiple killers
If ten people kill one person, each killer's 'Aqila pays one-tenth of the diyah, also over three years, because each part is treated like the whole.
This is based on qiyas: part as whole.
5
Note the killer's own liability
In cases like a father killing his son intentionally (where retaliation is converted to diyah), the obligation is also deferred three years, not immediate.
This is an extension of the principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : (وهي أهل الديوان إن كان القاتل منهم تؤخذ من عطاياهم في ثلاث سنين. وأهل الديوان هم الجيش الذين كتبت أسماؤهم في الديوان وهذا عندنا. وقال الشافعي: على أهل العشيرة لما روينا وكان كذلك إلى أيام . عمر رضي الله عنه ولا نسخ بعد النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم فيبقى على ما كان ولأنها صلة والأقارب أولى بها كالإرث والنفقات. ولنا أقضية عمر رضي الله عنه فإنه لما دون الدواوين جعل الدية على أهل الديوان بمحضر من الصحابة من غير نكير منهم وليس ذلك بنسخ بل هو تقرير معنى لأنه كان على أهل النصرة وقد . بأنواع بالحلف والولاء والعدو وفي عهد عمر رضي الله عنه قد صارت بالديوان فجعل على أهلها اتباعاً للمعنى وهذا قالوا لو كان اليوم يتناصرون بالحرف فعاقلتهم أهل الحرفة، وإن كانوا بالحف فأهله والدية صلة كما قال لكن إيجابها فيما وهو العطار أولى من إيجابها هو في أصول أموالهم لأن أحق وما تحملت العاقلة إلا للتخفيف القاتل في ماله كما إذا قتل الأب ابنه عمداً ليس بحال عندنا بل مؤجلاً بثلاث سنين فتأمل هل يمكن دفعه؟ وهذا إذا كانت العطايا للسنين المستقبلة حتى لو اجتمعت في السنين الماضية قبل القضاء بالدية ثم خرجت بعد القضاء لا يؤخذ منها لأن الوجوب بالقضاء، ولو خرجت عطايا ثلاث سنين مستقبلة في سنة واحدة يؤخذ منها كل الدية لأنها بعد الوجوب إذ الوجوب بالقضاء وقد حصل المقصود بذلك وهو التخفيف، وإذا كان الواجب ثلث الدية أو أقل يجب في سنة واحدة، وإذا كان أكثر منه يجب في سنتين إلى تمام الثلثين، ثم إذا كان أكثر منه إلى تمام الدية تجب في ثلاث سنين لأن جمع الدية في ثلاث سنين فيكون كل ثلث في سنة ضرورة، والواجب على القاتل كالواجب على العاقلة حتى تجب في ثلاث سنين وذلك مثل الأب إذا قتل ابنه عمداً إذا انقلب القصاص مالاً. ولو قتل عشرة رجلاً واحداً خطأ فعلى عاقلة كل واحد منهم عشر الدية في ثلاث سنين اعتباراً للجزء بالكل وهو He (the author) said, may Allah have mercy on him: (And they are the people of the diwan if the killer is from among them; it is taken from their stipends in three years. The people of the diwan are the army whose names are written in the diwan, and this is our position. Al-Shafi'i said: On the people of the tribe, as we have narrated, and it was like that until the time of 'Umar, may Allah be pleased with him, and there is no repeal after the Prophet, peace be upon him, so it remains as it was, and because it is a connection, and relatives are more deserving of it like inheritance and maintenance. Our proof is the rulings of 'Umar, may Allah be pleased with him, for when he organized the diwans, he placed the diyah on the people of the diwan in the presence of the Companions without any objection from them. That is not a repeal but a confirmation of the meaning, because it was originally on the people of support, and they used to support each other through alliances, clientage, and enmity, but in the time of 'Umar, support came through the diwan, so he placed it on its people following the meaning. They said: If today they support each other through a craft, then their 'Aqila would be the people of that craft; if through a group, then its people. And the diyah is a connection as he said, but imposing it on one's trade goods is more appropriate than imposing it on his capital, because he is more entitled, and the 'Aqila only assumed liability for alleviation. The estimation of three years is reported from the Prophet, peace be upon him, and narrated from 'Umar, may Allah be pleased with him, end. He (the author) said, may Allah have mercy on him: (If the stipends come out in more years or less, it is taken from them to achieve the intended goal, because the goal is alleviation and it has been achieved. Three? I say: there is a discussion, that analogy would reject imposing wealth in exchange for a sacred soul due to lack of equivalence, except that the Law has come with that as they stated. The Law only came with imposing it deferred for three years, for it is reported from the Prophet, peace be upon him, and narrated from 'Umar, may Allah be pleased with him, as previously. So the deferment should be restricted to three years, since it is established with them that the ruling that comes contrary to analogy is confined to what is mentioned. A similar example will come in the book regarding the explanation that what is obligated on the killer from his wealth, like when a father kills his son intentionally, is not immediate in our school but deferred for three years; consider if it can be refuted. This is if the stipends are for future years, such that if they accrued in past years before the judgment of diyah and then came out after judgment, nothing is taken from them because the obligation is by judgment. If the stipends of three future years come out in one year, all the diyah is taken from them because it is after the obligation, as the obligation is by judgment, and the intended alleviation has been achieved. If the obligatory amount is one third of the diyah or less, it is due in one year; if more, it is due in two years up to two-thirds; then if more than that up to the full diyah, it is due in three years, because the collection of the diyah is over three years, so each third is necessarily in one year. The obligation on the killer is like the obligation on the 'Aqila, so it is due in three years; that is like the father who kills his son intentionally when retaliation is converted to property. If ten persons kill one person by mistake, then on the 'Aqila of each of them is one-tenth of the diyah in three years, considering the part as the whole, and it is the substitute for the soul, so each part is deferred for three years. The beginning of the period is considered from the time of judgment of the diyah, because the original obligation is the diyah and its conversion to value. Allah: (And if there is no diwan, then on his 'Aqila) as we have narrated at that time. He said, may Allah have mercy on him: (And it is divided among them in three years; not more than one dirham or one and one-third dirham is taken from each person in each year, and not exceeding from each person from the whole diyah in
Source pages p. 204

Commentary
This passage details the practical ruling on how diyah is collected from the 'Aqila, specifically when the killer belongs to the diwan (military register). The Hanafi school holds that the diyah is taken from the stipends of the diwan members over three years. This is based on reports from the Prophet and the practice of 'Umar. The text addresses several scenarios: if the stipends are paid over more or fewer than three years, the diyah is adjusted accordingly to achieve the goal of alleviation. If the stipends of three future years come out in one year, all the diyah is taken at once because the obligation is by judicial judgment and the purpose is served. The amount taken per person per year is limited: not more than one dirham or one and one-third dirham, and the total taken from one person over three years does not exceed four dirhams (the text is cut off but it implies a maximum). The ruling also applies to the killer himself in certain cases, like a father killing his son intentionally when retaliation is converted to diyah; even then, it is deferred over three years. If multiple killers are involved, each killer's 'Aqila bears a proportional share, also deferred over three years. The period starts from the time of judicial judgment, not from the killing, because the obligation is established by judgment. The underlying principle is that this is a concession contrary to analogy, so it is strictly limited to what the texts specify. The text also mentions that if today people support each other through a craft or profession, then the 'Aqila would be the people of that craft, illustrating that the basis is mutual support, not fixed blood relations.

Key Terms
العطايا (al-'ataya): The stipends paid to soldiers registered in the diwan.
التأجيل (al-ta'jil): The deferment of diyah payment over three years for alleviation.
القضاء (al-qada'): The judicial judgment that establishes the obligation of diyah.
كل الثلثين (kull al-thulthayn): Up to two-thirds of diyah, which triggers a two-year collection.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If the diyah is one-sixth of the full amount, over how many years is it collected?
A) One year
B) Two years
C) Three years
D) Six years

Key Takeaway
The three-year deferment is a maximum; smaller diyah amounts are collected over one or two years proportionally.

Extending the 'Aqila: Closest Tribes and Per-Person Limit
Expounds ruling on extending 'aqila, per-person amount, and cross-madhhab difference

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi rules for extending the 'aqila and determine the per-person amount for diya, distinguishing between Arab and non-Arab cases and between Hanafi and Shafi'i positions.

Core Idea
The 'aqila is extended to the closest tribes in order of 'asabaat, and no one pays more than 4 dirhams per year.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains what happens when a tribe cannot bear the full diya for an accidental killing. The Hanafi school outlines a systematic extension to the nearest male relatives, caps individual contributions to avoid hardship, and addresses practical differences between Arab and non-Arab communities. It also contrasts with the Shafi'i view and provides guidance for collecting diya from stipend recipients.

Guided Path
1
State the rule for extending the 'aqila
Identify the matn rule: if the immediate tribe cannot pay, add the closest tribes according to the order of 'asabaat (paternal male relatives). This is to spread the burden fairly.
The order follows the inheritance order of 'asabaat.
2
Analyze the intra-Hanafi disagreement on including father and sons
Some Hanafi scholars include the killer's father and sons because of close relation; others exclude them because the goal is to have many contributors, but fathers and sons are few.
The excluded view is considered stronger for the purpose of alleviation.
3
Differentiate between Arab and non-Arab cases
For Arabs, lineage is well-known, so extension is by tribe. For non-Arabs, who have lost track of lineages, the matter is left to the Imam's discretion or the closest locality.
This reflects flexibility in applying the ruling.
4
Compare Hanafi and Shafi'i views on per-person payment
Hanafi limits each person to 4 dirhams per year (total over 3 years). Shafi'i requires each person to pay half a dinar equally, analogizing to zakat.
The Hanafi view prioritizes avoiding hardship over equal distribution.
5
Understand collection from stipend recipients
If 'aqila are on military stipends, diya is taken from their annual grants (a'tiya) over three years, not from monthly subsistence stipends (arzaq), to avoid harming their daily needs.
This shows consideration for the payer's circumstances.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لأن قال رحمه الله : (فإن لم تتسع القبيلة لذلك ضم إليها أقرب القبائل نسباً على ترتيب العصبات) لتحقق معنى التخفيف واختلفوا في أبي القاتل وأبنائه قيل يدخلون لقربهم، وقيل لا يدخلون لأن الضم ينفي الحرج حتى لا يصيب كل واحد أكثر من أربعة، وهذا المعنى إنما يستحق عند الكثرة والأبناء والآباء لا يكثرون قالوا هذا في حق العرب لأنهم حفظوا أنسابهم فأمكن إيجابهم على أقرب القبائل، وأما العجم فقد ضيعوا أنسابهم فلا يمكن ذلك في حقهم فإذا لم يكن فقد اختلفوا فيه؛ فقال بعضهم يعتبر بالمحال والقرية الأقرب فالأقرب، وقال بعضهم رأي يفوض ذلك إلى الإمام لأنه هو العالم به وهذا كله عندنا، وعند الإمام الشافعي يجب على كل واحد نصف دينار فيستوي بين الكل لأنه كله صلة فيعتبر بالزكاة. ولو كانت عاقلته أصحاب الرزق يقضى بالدية في أرزاقهم في ثلاث سنين في كل سنة الثلث يؤخذ كلما خرج رزق ثلث الدية بمنزلة العطايا، وإن كان يخرج في كل سنة وأرزاق في شهر فرضت الدية في الأعطية دون الأرزاق لأن الأخذ من الأعطية أيسر لهم والأخذ من الأرزاق يؤدي إلى الإضرار بهم إذ الأرزاق لكفاية الوقت ويتضررون وبالأداء منه والأعطية ليكونوا مؤتلفين في الديوان قائمين بالنصرة فتيسر عليهم الأداء منه
Because he (the author) said, may Allah have mercy on him: (If the tribe does not suffice for that, then the closest tribes in lineage are added to it according to the order of 'asabaat) to achieve the meaning of alleviation. And they differed regarding the killer's father and his sons: some say they are included due to their closeness, and some say they are not included because the addition removes hardship so that no one incurs more than four, but this meaning is only relevant when there are many; fathers and sons are not many. They said this applies to Arabs because they preserved their lineages; thus it is possible to impose on the closest tribes. As for non-Arabs, they have lost their lineages, so that is not possible regarding them. If there is no lineage, they differed: some said one considers the closest locality and village, and some said the opinion is delegated to the Imam because he is the one knowledgeable of it. All this is according to us. According to Imam al-Shafi'i, each person must pay half a dinar, so all are equal because it is all a form of connection, so it is analogized to zakat. If his 'aqila are stipend recipients, the blood money is decreed upon their stipends over three years: each year one-third is taken; whenever a stipend is disbursed, one-third of the blood money is taken, similar to grants. If there are annual disbursements but stipends for a month, the blood money is imposed on the grants (a'tiya) and not on the stipends (arzaq) because taking from grants is easier for them; taking from stipends leads to harm because stipends are for daily needs and they would be harmed by paying from them, whereas grants are for uniting them in the military register (diwan) and for supporting them, so payment from grants is facilitated.
Source pages p. 206

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with explanatory reasoning (talil) regarding the extension of the 'aqila when the immediate tribe cannot bear the blood money (diya). The matn states that if the tribe is insufficient, the closest tribes in lineage are added in the order of 'asabaat (paternal male relatives). The commentary explains that this is to achieve alleviation (takhfif) so that no individual pays more than four dirhams per year. There is an intra-Hanafi difference about including the killer's father and sons: some say they are included because they are close relatives, others say they are not because the purpose is to spread the burden among many, and fathers and sons are few. The passage then distinguishes between Arabs, who have preserved lineages, and non-Arabs, who have not; for the latter, the matter is left to the Imam's discretion. It then notes that Imam al-Shafi'i holds that each 'aqila member pays half a dinar, equalizing all, because he analogizes it to zakat. Finally, it discusses the case where the 'aqila are stipend recipients (ashab al-rizq): the diya is collected from their stipends over three years, but if they receive both annual grants (a'tiya) and monthly stipends (arzaq), the diya is taken from the grants only, as that is easier and less harmful.

Key Terms
العاقلة (al-'āqila): The group of male relatives (tribe or clan) responsible for paying blood money (diya) on behalf of a perpetrator of accidental killing.
العصبات (al-'aṣabāt): In this context, the agnatic male relatives (paternal relations) in order of priority, used to determine which tribes are added when the original 'aqila is insufficient.
النصرة (al-nuṣra): Mutual support and defense within the tribe or clan; the basis for imposing diya on the 'aqila.
العطية (al-'aṭiyya): Annual grant or bonus given to stipend recipients, typically from the military register (diwan).
الرزق (al-rizq): Monthly subsistence stipend for daily needs.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the maximum amount that can be taken from a single member of the 'aqila per year for diya?
A) 1 dirham
B) 2 dirhams
C) 4 dirhams
D) Half a dinar

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi 'aqila system extends to the nearest tribes in order of 'asabaat, caps individual contribution at 4 dirhams per year, and adapts to the means of the community (tribal lineage or ruler's discretion).

Killer's Obligation in 'Aqila: Hanafi vs. Shafi'i
States that the killer is like one of the 'aqila and rebuts Shafi'i objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position that the killer contributes to the diya as part of the 'aqila and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument.

Core Idea
The killer must pay his share in the 'aqila because the obligation is based on mutual support, not on innocence.

This lesson examines a classic dispute between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools: should the person who accidentally kills be required to contribute to the blood money (diya) along with the 'aqila? The Hanafi school answers yes, while Imam al-Shafi'i argues the killer is excused entirely. Understanding this difference reveals how each school weighs the principles of justice, mutual support, and the limits of financial obligation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hanafi position
State clearly what the Hanafi school holds: the killer is like one of the 'aqila and must pay his share of the diya.
This is found in the matn statement: 'And the killer is like one of them.'
2
Understand the Shafi'i objection
Imam al-Shafi'i argues that since the killer is excused (ma'dhur), no part of the diya should be due from him; the part follows the whole.
His reasoning is based on analogy: if the full diya is not imposed, a part should not be imposed either.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response
The Hanafi school gives two responses: (1) imposing the whole would be excessive, but a part is not; (2) the obligation is based on mutual support (nasra), and the killer is the strongest supporter of himself, so he is more obligated.
The response also addresses the Quranic verse about bearing burdens, arguing it is not violated.
4
Evaluate the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf)
Under Hanafi law, the killer pays a share; under Shafi'i, the 'aqila pays entirely without contribution from the killer.
This shows how the disagreement affects actual financial liability.
5
Formulate the tarjih (preferred view)
The Hanafi position is presented as the relied-upon view in the text, based on stronger reasoning regarding nasra and proportionality.
Implicitly, the author sides with the Hanafi view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله: (والقاتل كأحدهم) أي كواحد من العاقلة فلا معنى لإخراجه ومؤاخذة غيره به. وقال الشافعي رضي الله عنه : لا يجب على القاتل شيء من الدية لأنه معذور ولهذا لا يجب عليه الكل فكذا البعض إذا الجزء لا يخالف الكل. قلنا ايجاب الكل إجحاف به ولا كذلك إيجاب البعض ولأنها تجب بالنصرة ولا ينصر نفسه مثل ما ينصر غيره بل أشده فكان أولى بالايجاب عليه، فإذا كان المخطئ معذوراً فالبريء منه أولى قال الله تعالى: ﴿ولا تزر وازرة وزر أخرى . [الانعام : ١٦٤]
He (the author) said, may Allah have mercy on him: (And the killer is like one of them) meaning like one of the 'aqila, so there is no meaning to excluding him and holding others responsible instead of him. Al-Shafi'i, may Allah be pleased with him, said: Nothing of the blood money is due from the killer because he is excused; therefore the whole amount is not due from him, so neither is a part, since the part does not differ from the whole. We respond: Imposing the whole amount on him would be excessive, but imposing a part is not. Also, it is due based on mutual support (nasra), and he does not support himself as he supports others; rather, he supports others more intensely, so he is more entitled to be obligated. If the one who erred is excused, then the one who is innocent is more deserving [of being excused]? But Allah says: And no bearer of burdens shall bear the burden of another (Al-An'am 6:164).
Source pages p. 206

Commentary
This passage presents an inter-madhhab dispute between the Hanafi school and Imam al-Shafi'i. The matn states that the killer himself is counted as one of the 'aqila, meaning he must contribute to the blood money (diya) along with the others. Al-Shafi'i argues that the killer should be excused entirely because he is already burdened by the accidental killing; if the full diya is not due from him, then a part should not be due either. The Hanafi response relies on two points: first, imposing the full diya would be unjust (ijhaf), but a partial payment is not; second, the obligation is based on mutual support (nasra), and the killer is the one who most needs and gives support, so he is the most deserving of being obligated. The Hanafi school further argues that the verse 'No bearer shall bear another's burden' supports their view: the killer's burden is not being transferred to others; he is simply contributing alongside them.

Key Terms
القاتل (al-qātil): The person who caused the accidental killing; he is considered part of the 'aqila in Hanafi law.
النصرة (al-nuṣra): Mutual support; the legal basis for the 'aqila obligation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the Hanafi response to the Shafi'i argument that the killer should not pay anything because he is excused?
A) The killer must pay the full diya alone.
B) Imposing the full diya would be excessive, but a part is not.
C) The killer is not excused at all.
D) The 'aqila pays nothing and the killer pays everything.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school includes the killer in the 'aqila because the obligation is based on mutual support, and a partial payment is not excessive.

'Aqila of a Freed Slave and a Client of Muwalat
Defines the 'aqila for two types of clients.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state who constitutes the 'aqila for a freed slave and for a client of wala' al-muwalat, and explain the legal basis for each ruling.

Core Idea
The 'aqila of a freed slave and a muwalat client is the patron's tribe, not their own kin.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies the 'aqila for two types of protected persons: a freed slave and a client of muwalat. Understanding this helps identify who bears responsibility for blood money when such a person commits homicide.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories
The text discusses the 'aqila for a freed slave (mawla al-itaq) and a client of wala' al-muwalat (mawla al-muwalat). Recognize that both lack a natural tribe for support.
The freed slave is connected to the patron who freed him; the muwalat client contracts an alliance with a patron.
2
State the ruling for each
For the freed slave, the 'aqila is the tribe of the patron. For the muwalat client, the 'aqila is his patron (the one who contracted the muwalat) and that patron's tribe.
The word 'qabilatuhu' in the text refers to the patron's tribe.
3
Examine the proof
The text cites the hadith 'Mawla al-qawmi minhum' (The client of a people is one of them) to justify treating the client as part of the patron's tribe.
This hadith is a principle of affiliation (nasra) in Hanafi fiqh.
4
Understand the analogy
The muwalat client is likened to the freed slave: both are attached to a patron for protection, so their 'aqila follows the patron's ties.
The text explicitly says 'fashabbaha' (he resembles) the freed slave's client.
5
Apply the ruling
In practice, if either a freed slave or a muwalat client commits accidental homicide, the blood money (diya) must be paid by the designated 'aqila, not by any biological relatives.
This ensures that the responsibility falls on those who have authority over the individual.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : ( وعاقلة المعتق قبيلة مولاه) إذ نصرته واسمها بهم ينبي عنها يؤيد ذلك قوله صلى الله عليه وسلم مولى القوم منهم قال رحمه الله ويعقل عن مولى الموالاة مولاه وقبيلته ومولى الموالاة هو الحليف فيعقل عنه مولاه الذي عاقده وعاقلة مولاه وهو المراد بقوله «وقبيلته أي قبيلة مولاه الذي عاقده لأنه المعروف به فأشبه مولى العتاقة.
He (the author) said, may Allah have mercy on him: (And the 'aqila of the freed slave is the tribe of his patron) because his support and his affiliation are indicated by them. This is supported by his statement, peace be upon him: “The client of a people is one of them.” He (the author) said, may Allah have mercy on him: And the client of wala' al-muwalat is responsible through his patron and his tribe. The client of muwalat is the ally; thus his patron with whom he contracted and the 'aqila of his patron are responsible for him. This is what is meant by his words “and his tribe” i.e., the tribe of his patron with whom he contracted, because he is known by him, so he resembles the freed slave's client.
Source pages p. 206

Commentary
This passage states two rulings concerning the 'aqila. First, for a freed slave (mawla al-itaq), the 'aqila is the tribe of the patron who freed him. This is because the freed slave's support and identity are tied to the patron's tribe, and it is supported by the hadith “The client of a people is one of them.” Second, for a client of wala' al-muwalat (a contractual client), the 'aqila is his own patron (the one who contracted the muwalat) and that patron's tribe. The client of muwalat is essentially an ally who has no family; he contracts with a person for mutual support. The text explains that such a client's 'aqila is the same as the patron's 'aqila, by analogy with the freed slave.

Key Terms
مولى العتاقة (mawlā al-ʿitāqa): A freed slave; his 'aqila is the tribe of the patron who freed him.
مولى الموالاة (mawlā al-muwālāt): A client who contracts wala' al-muwalat (a contractual alliance) with a patron for mutual inheritance and support. His 'aqila is the patron and the patron's tribe.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
For a client of wala' al-muwalat, who is his 'aqila?
A) His own biological tribe
B) The tribe of the patron who contracted muwalat with him
C) The freed slave's tribe
D) The Imam's diwan

Key Takeaway
The 'aqila of both a freed slave and a client of muwalat is the patron's tribe, based on the principle of protection and the prophetic hadith.

What the 'Aqila Does Not Bear and the Role of Confession and Evidence
Ruling statement on exclusions to 'aqila liability

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the four cases the 'aqila does not bear and explain when a confession or settlement becomes binding on them.

Core Idea
The 'aqila only bears diya for accidental killing; confession and settlement bind only the killer unless affirmed or proven by evidence.

The 'aqila is a group of male relatives who pay blood money (diya) for accidental killings. However, not all financial obligations from a homicide fall on them. This passage clarifies four exclusions: injuries by a slave, intentional killing, obligations from reconciliation (sulh), and obligations from confession (iqrar). It then provides two exceptions where the 'aqila may still be liable: if they affirm the confession or if evidence (bayyina) establishes the obligation. Understanding these nuances is crucial for correct application of diya law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Four Excluded Cases
List the four cases the 'aqila does not bear: 1) injury by a slave, 2) intentional killing, 3) what is owed via reconciliation (sulh), 4) what is owed via confession (iqrar).
These are based on the principle that the 'aqila's authority (nasra) is limited.
2
Understand the Rationale
The reason for excluding confession and reconciliation is that the killer has no authority over the 'aqila to bind them; these are personal acts of the killer.
The 'aqila is not a guarantor for all depts, only for standard accidental diya.
3
Note the Two Exceptions
The 'aqila becomes liable if they affirm the confession (tasadduq) or if the obligation is established by clear evidence (bayyina).
Affirmation is like a confession from the 'aqila themselves.
4
Distinguish Procedural Effects
Diya based on confession is payable in installments over three years starting from judgment; diya based on reconciliation is immediate unless deferred by stipulation.
Evidence-based diya also starts from judgment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : (ولا تعقل عاقلة جناية العبد ولا العمد وما لزم صلحاً واعترافاً لما روينا ولأنه لا ينتصر بالعبد والإقرار والصلح لا يلزمان العاقلة لقصور ولايته عنهم. قال رحمه الله (إلا أن يصدقوه في الإقرار لأن التصديق إقرار منهم فتلزمهم بإقرارهم بأن لهم ولاية على أنفسهم والامتناع كان لحقهم وقد زال أو تقوم البينة لأن ما ثبت بالبينة كالمشاهدة لأنها كاسمها مبينة، وتقبل البينة هنا الاقرار وإن كانت لا تعتبر معه لأنها تثبت ما ليس بثابت باقرار المدعى مع عليه وهو الوجوب على العاقلة، ثم ما ثبت بالإقرار يجب مؤجلاً وما ثبت بالصلح حال إلا إذا شرط التأجيل في الصلح وقد عرف في موضعه. ولو أقر بالقتل خطأ فلم يرتفعوا إلى الحاكم إلا بعد سنين فقضي عليه بالدية في ماله في ثلاث سنين كان أول المدة من يوم قضي عليه لأن التأجيل من وقت القضاء في الثابت بالبينة فكذا في الثابت بالإقرار أولى لأنه أضعف. ولو تصادق القاتل وأولياء المقتول على أن قاضي بلد كذا قضى بالدية على عاقلته بالبينة وكذبتهما العاقلة فلا شيء على العاقلة لأن تصادقهما لا يكون حجة عليهم ولم يكن عليه شيء في ماله لأن الدية بتصادقهما تقررت على العاقلة بالقضاء وتصادقهما حجة في حقهما فلا يلزم إلا حصته بخلاف الأول حيث تجب جميع الدية على المقر لأنه لم يوجد التصديق من الولي بالقضاء بالدية على العاقلة وقد وجد هنا فافترقا .
He (the author) said, may Allah have mercy on him: (And the 'aqila does not bear the injury of a slave, nor intentional killing, nor what is obligated by reconciliation or confession) because, as we have narrated, the slave does not receive support, and confession and reconciliation do not bind the 'aqila due to the deficiency of their authority over them. He (the author) said, may Allah have mercy on him: (Except if they affirm the confession) because affirmation is a confession from them, so it becomes binding upon them by their own confession, as they have authority over themselves; the prohibition was for their right, and it has ceased. Or if evidence is established, because what is established by evidence is like eyewitnessing, for evidence is clarifying as its name implies. Evidence is accepted here despite the confession, even though it is not considered with it, because it establishes what is not established by the confession of the defendant, namely the obligation upon the 'aqila. Then what is established by confession is due in installments, and what is established by reconciliation is due immediately unless deferment is stipulated in the reconciliation, as is known in its place. If someone confessed to accidental killing and they did not go to the judge until after years, and then he was ordered to pay the blood money from his own wealth in three years, the period starts from the day of the ruling because the deferment is from the time of the ruling for what is established by evidence; similarly for what is established by confession, even more so because it is weaker. If the killer and the heirs of the victim agree that the judge of such a city decreed the blood money against his 'aqila by evidence, but the 'aqila deny this, then nothing is due from the 'aqila because their agreement is not proof against them, and nothing is due from the killer's own wealth because the blood money was determined to be on the 'aqila by the decree, and their agreement is proof in their own favor; so only his share is due. This differs from the first case where the full blood money is due from the confessor because there was no confirmation from the guardian regarding the decree against the 'aqila, whereas here there is, so they differ.
Source pages p. 206

Commentary
This passage explains four cases that the 'aqila does not bear: (1) injury caused by a slave (the slave's owner is responsible), (2) intentional killing (the killer's own liability or qisas), (3) what is settled by reconciliation (sulh) without court ruling, and (4) what is based solely on the killer's confession. The reason for excluding confession and reconciliation is that the 'aqila has no authority over the killer to make such admissions. However, there is an exception: if the 'aqila themselves affirm the confession (by declaring it true), then the obligation becomes binding on them because they have confessed. Alternatively, if the obligation is proven by evidence (bayyina), it also binds the 'aqila. The passage then details procedural differences: a confession results in installment payments starting from the judgment date, while reconciliation demands immediate payment unless otherwise stipulated. It also discusses a scenario where the killer and heirs agree that a judge decreed diya on the 'aqila by evidence, but the 'aqila deny it; in that case, only the killer's share is due from him, not the full amount, because their agreement does not bind the 'aqila.

Key Terms
الإقرار (al-iqrār): A confession made by the killer alone, which does not bind the 'aqila unless they affirm it.
الصلح (al-ṣulḥ): A settlement between the killer and heirs for a sum of money, which is not binding on the 'aqila.
البينة (al-bayyina): Legal evidence (usually two witnesses) that can establish the obligation on the 'aqila.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Under which condition does the 'aqila become liable for diya based on the killer's confession?
A) Always, if the confession is true.
B) Only if the 'aqila themselves affirm the confession.
C) Never, because confession is the killer's personal liability.
D) Only if the killer is a slave.

Key Takeaway
The 'aqila is only liable for diya in accidental killing; four exclusions remove this liability, but it can be reinstated if the 'aqila affirms a confession or if evidence is established.

'Aqila Bears Diya for Free Person's Accidental Killing
Explains that the 'aqila bears diya for accidental killing of a free person and refutes the Shafi'i view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on 'aqila's liability for accidental killing and explain the difference with Imam al-Shafi'i's view, including the thamarah al-khilaf.

Core Idea
The 'aqila pays diya for accidental killing because it compensates a human life, not property.

In this lesson, we examine a key dispute between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools: who bears the financial responsibility for the accidental killing of a free person? The Hanafi school places this on the 'aqila (the victim's male relatives), while Shafi'i places it on the killer's own wealth. Understanding the reasoning behind this difference sharpens your grasp of the nature of diya and the role of the 'aqila in Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and the act
Identify the perpetrator (a free person), the act (accidental killing), and the liable party ('aqila).
The 'aqila is the group of male relatives who traditionally bear diya.
2
State the Hanafi position and Shafi'i objection
State clearly that Hanafis hold the 'aqila liable for diya; Shafi'i holds the killer personally liable.
3
Examine the Hanafi dalil
The victim is a human (adami); diya is a substitute for a human, not property. This distinguishes it from injuries below the soul.
The hadith 'The 'aqila does not bear the injury of a slave nor an intentional injury' is interpreted as excluding only those two.
4
Analyze the hadith interpretation
The Hanafi school accepts the hadith but interprets it narrowly: the 'aqila is exempt only for slaves (property) and intentional injury (personal liability).
This leaves accidental killing of free persons under 'aqila liability.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: under Hanafi law, the 'aqila pays; under Shafi'i, the killer pays from his own wealth.
This affects financial responsibility in real cases.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : (وإن جنى حر على خطأ فهي على عاقلته) يعني إذا قتله لأن العاقلة لا تتحمل أطراف العبد وقال الشافعي: لا تتحمل النفس أيضاً بل يجب في مال القاتل. وننا أنه آدمي فتتحمله العاقلة كالحر وهذا لأن ما يجب قتل دية وهي بدل الآدمي لا المال على ما بيناه من قبل فكانت على العاقلة بخلاف ما دون النفس لأنه يسلك به مسلك الأموال، والمراد بالحديث قوله صلى الله عليه وسلم لا تعقل العاقلة عبداً ولا عمداً جناية» أي لا تعقل العاقلة جناية عمداً ولا جناية عبد ونحن نقول به لأن جنايته توجب دفعة إلا أن يفدية المولى.
He (the author) said, may Allah have mercy on him: (And if a free person commits an accidental injury, then it is upon his 'aqila) meaning if he killed him, because the 'aqila does not bear injuries of a slave. Al-Shafi'i said: They also do not bear killing a soul; rather it must be from the killer's wealth. Our answer: He is a human, so the 'aqila bears it like a free person. This is because what is required is blood money (diya) as compensation for a human being, not for property, as we explained before. So it is upon the 'aqila, unlike injuries below the soul because those are treated as property. The meaning of the hadith is his statement, peace be upon him: “The 'aqila does not bear the injury of a slave nor an intentional injury.” i.e., the 'aqila does not bear an intentional injury nor an injury of a slave. And we adhere to this because the injury of a slave requires immediate payment unless the master ransoms him.
Source pages p. 207

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute regarding whether the 'aqila bears blood money (diya) for the accidental killing of a free person. The matn states that if a free person commits an accidental injury, the diya is on the 'aqila of the perpetrator, specifically for causing death. Imam al-Shafi'i argues that the 'aqila does not bear diya for taking a life; rather, it must be from the killer's own wealth. The Hanafi response is based on the fact that the victim is a human being (adami), so the compensation is diya (blood money) as a substitute for a human, not as property. This is distinct from injuries below the soul (i.e., manslaughter causing death is treated differently from property damages). The text also clarifies the hadith 'The 'aqila does not bear the injury of a slave nor an intentional injury' – the Hanafis accept this hadith and interpret it as excluding only those two categories: injuries to slaves (because slaves are property) and intentional injuries (which lead to qisas or are the killer's own liability).

Key Terms
النفس (al-nafs): A human soul; killing a free person is considered more grave and is compensated by diya which is on the 'aqila.
ما دون النفس (mā dūn al-nafs): Injuries less than death (e.g., wounds). These are treated as property compensation and are the responsibility of the perpetrator, not the 'aqila.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, why does the 'aqila bear diya for accidental killing but not for injuries below the soul?
A) Because killing a soul is a capital crime.
B) Because diya for death is a substitute for a human being, while injuries are treated as property.
C) Because the hadith specifically exempts injuries below the soul.
D) Because the killer is more culpable for death.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that the 'aqila bears diya for accidental killing of a free person because the compensation is for a human life, not property, unlike injuries which are treated as property.

Women and Children Not Liable for 'Aqilah
States the Hanafi ruling on women and children in 'aqila.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state when women and children are excluded from 'aqila liability, apply the diwan-based determination, and identify the exception when they directly cause the killing.

Core Idea
Women and children are not in 'aqila unless they directly cause the killing.

The Hanafi school establishes that the 'aqila system is based on mutual support (nasra) among those capable of military service. Women and children are not obligated to contribute to blood money because they do not provide such support. However, this lesson explores the nuance: if a woman or child directly commits the accidental killing, the correct Hanafi view is that they do participate in the 'aqila. We also examine how the diwan register overrides lineage in organized states.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule
The Hanafi school states women and children are not required to pay 'aqil. Note the two proofs: the statement of 'Umar and the principle of nasra.
The rule applies to those who have a share in the diwan but that is not a condition.
2
Recognize the exception for direct causation
If a woman or child directly commits the killing (mubashara), the correct view is that they join the 'aqila. This includes a madman who directly kills.
The intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf: some early views may have excluded them completely; the text affirms the exception.
3
Understand the diwan's role
In a state with a military register, the 'aqila is based on the diwan, not lineage. Two brothers in different diwans do not bear for each other.
The diwan takes precedence over proximity of residence, alliance, or kinship.
4
Apply the diwan rule to a resident without stipend
If a person resides in a city but has no stipend in the diwan, the people of the city's diwan bear his 'aqil, even if his Bedouin relatives are closer in lineage.
Kinship is not a condition for diwan-based 'aqila.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال أصحابنا : ليس على المرأة والذرية ممن له حظ في الديوان عقل لقول عمر رضي الله عنه : لا يعقل مع العواقل صبي ولا امرأة ولأن العقل إنما يجب على أهل النصرة والناس لا يتناصرون بالصبيان والنساء ولهذا لا يوضع عليهم ما خلف عن النصرة وهو الجزية، وعلى هذا لو كان القاتل صبياً أو امرأة لا شيء عليهما من الدية وهذا صحيح فيما إذا قتله غيرهما، وأما إذا باشرا القتل بأنفسهما فالصحيح أنهما يشاركان العاقلة، وكذا المجنون إذا قتل فالصحيح أن يكون كواحد من العاقلة والحاصل أن الاستنصار بالديوان أظهر فلا يظهر معه حكم النصرة بالقرابة والولاء وقرب السكنى والعدو الحلف وبعد الديوان النصرة بالنسب على ما بينا، وعلى هذا يخرج كثيراً من مسائل المعاقل . أخوان ديوان أحدهما بالبصرة وديوان الثاني بالكوفة لا يعقل أحدهما صاحبه إنما يعقل عنه أهل ديوانه ومن جنى جناية من أهل عن البصرة وليس له في أهل الديوان عطاء وأهل البادية أقرب إليه نسباً ومسكنه المصر عقل عنه أهل الديوان . ذلك المصر، ولم يشترط أن يكون بينه وبين أهل الديوان قرابة لأن أهل من هو
Our companions said: There is no 'aqil obligation on women and children among those who have a share in the military register (diwan) due to the statement of 'Umar, may Allah be pleased with him: “No male child or woman bears 'aqil with the 'aqila.” And because 'aqil is only obligatory upon those capable of mutual support, and people do not support each other through boys and women. Therefore, they are not subject to what is left of support, which is jizya. Based on this, if the killer is a boy or a woman, nothing of the blood money is due from them; this is correct in the case where someone else killed. However, if they directly committed the killing themselves, the correct view is that they participate with the 'aqila. Similarly, if a madman kills, the correct view is that he is like one of the 'aqila. The conclusion is that reliance on the diwan is more apparent, so the ruling of support by lineage, patronage, proximity of residence, and the enemy alliance does not appear with it. When the diwan is absent, the support is by lineage as we explained. Based on this many issues of 'aqil are derived: Two brothers, one in the diwan of Basra and the other in the diwan of Kufa, do not bear 'aqil for each other; only the people of his own diwan bear 'aqil for him. If a person from among the people of Basra commits an injury and he has no stipend among the people of the diwan, and the Bedouins are closer to him in lineage but his residence is the city, then the people of the diwan of that city bear 'aqil for him. It is not required that there be kinship between him and the people of the diwan because the people of ... is
Source pages p. 207

Commentary
This passage concludes the discussion of 'aqila by clarifying who is not included. The Hanafi school (as expressed by 'our companions') states that women and children are not required to pay 'aqil, based on a report from 'Umar ibn al-Khattab and the principle that 'aqil is only for those who can provide mutual support (nasra). Women and children do not participate in fighting, so they are not included. However, if a woman or child directly commits the killing, the correct view is that they still participate with the 'aqila because they are the actual perpetrators. Similarly, a madman who kills is treated as one of the 'aqila. The passage then emphasizes that in a society organized by a military register (diwan), the 'aqila is determined by the diwan rather than by lineage. Thus, two brothers registered in different diwans do not bear 'aqil for each other; each is responsible to his own diwan. If a person has no stipend (ata') in the diwan but resides in a city, the people of that city's diwan bear 'aqil for him, even if there is no kinship. The text cuts off mid-sentence.

Key Terms
الذرية (al-dhurriyya): Children, specifically minors who are not liable for 'aqil.
الديوان (al-dīwān): The military register; in an organized state, the 'aqila is based on the diwan rather than lineage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a woman directly commits accidental killing, what is her status regarding the 'aqila?
A) She is completely excluded and pays nothing.
B) She participates with the 'aqila as one of them.
C) Her guardian pays on her behalf.
D) She must pay the full diya alone.

Key Takeaway
Women and children are not in 'aqila by default, but direct causation makes them participants.

Ruling on Converting 'Ata Wealth When Types Differ
Passage states the ruling when 'ata wealth is not of the same type as the judgment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on converting 'ata wealth when the payment type differs from the judgment and explain the reasoning behind preserving the original judgment.

Core Idea
If the judgment is for camels and 'ata is dirhams, do not convert the judgment; purchase camels with the dirhams.

In Hanafi fiqh, the 'ata (stipend) is a government allocation to the 'aqila for paying blood-money (diyah). But what happens when the qadi's judgment orders payment in camels, yet the 'ata available is in dirhams? This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies the ruling: the judgment cannot be converted to match the available type; instead, the camels must be purchased using the dirhams. This preserves the integrity of the original judgment while allowing practical fulfillment.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original judgment and the available 'ata type
The qadi ruled that the 'aqila must pay a specific number of camels. The 'ata (stipend) provided by the state is in dirhams, a different type of wealth.
2
Determine the ruling on converting the judgment
The passage states that the judgment does not convert to dirhams. The student must recognize that changing the type of payment would nullify the original judgment.
Conversion here means altering the judgment itself, not exchanging the payment.
3
Explain the reason for prohibition
Converting the judgment would invalidate the first judgment (ibṭāl al-qaḍāʾ al-awwal). The qadi's ruling must be respected as it was issued.
The principle is that a judgment cannot be altered unilaterally; it must be fulfilled as given.
4
State the correct procedure
The camels must be fulfilled from the 'ata wealth by purchasing them with the dirhams. This satisfies the judgment without changing its terms.
Purchasing is explicitly said to be 'easier' (aysar), implying it is a practical concession.
5
Note the underlying ease rationale
The passage mentions 'because that is easier' (li-annahu aysar). The Hanafi school considers facilitation while upholding the original obligation.
Ease does not override the prohibition of conversion; it merely justifies the method of purchase.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العطاء إلا إذا لم يكن مال العطاء من جنس ما قضى به عليهم بأن كان القضاء بالإبل والعطاء دراهم فحينئذ لا يتحول إلى الدراهم لما فيه من إبطال القضاء الأول لكن تقضى الإبل من مال العطايا بأن يشتري به لأنه أيسر .
The gift, except if the wealth of the gift is not of the type of what was adjudged against them, such as if the judgment was for camels and the gift was dirhams; then it does not convert to dirhams because that would nullify the first judgment, but the camels are fulfilled from the wealth of the gifts by purchasing them, because that is easier.
Source pages p. 209

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling concerning blood-money payment via 'ata (government stipends). The 'ata is a form of payment from the state treasury to the 'aqila (the clan responsible for paying blood-money). If the original judgment orders payment in camels, but the 'ata wealth available is in dirhams, the law does not permit converting the judgment to dirhams. Instead, the camels must be purchased from the dirhams of the 'ata. This is because changing the type of payment would effectively overturn the original judgment, which is not allowed. However, buying camels with the dirhams is permissible and easier. This ruling is in accordance with the Hanafi school's principle of preserving the original judgment's form.

Key Terms
العطاء (al-'ata): Government stipend or allowance given to the 'aqila from the treasury, used to pay blood-money.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a judgment orders payment in camels but the 'ata (stipend) is in dirhams, what must be done?
A) The dirhams are converted to camels at market rate
B) The camels are purchased using the dirhams
C) The judgment is changed to dirhams
D) The dirhams are returned and camels demanded

Key Takeaway
A qadi's judgment for a specific type of payment must be fulfilled in that type; if the available funds are of a different type, the required goods must be purchased, not the judgment changed.

Killer with No 'Aqila: Diyah from Bayt al-Mal and a Rare Narration
Ruling and qiyas on diyah from Bayt al-Mal

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the relied-upon Hanafi position on the source of diyah when a Muslim killer has no 'aqila, explain the qiyas used, and identify the rare narration as non-operative.

Core Idea
When a killer has no 'aqila, the Muslim community—via Bayt al-Mal—bears the blood-money, by analogy with inheritance.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies the fiqh of blood-money (diyah) for a Muslim killer who lacks a supporting clan ('aqila). The Hanafi school rules that the Bayt al-Mal (public treasury) is responsible, drawing a powerful analogy to Islamic inheritance law. However, a rare transmission (riwayah shadhdhah) from Imam Abu Hanifa himself suggests the killer's own wealth should be used—yet this minority view is not followed. Understanding how the school weighs transmissions is key to discerning the operative ruling.

Guided Path
1
State the main Hanafi ruling
Extract the ruling from the text: if a Muslim killer has no 'aqila, the diyah is upon Bayt al-Mal. Note that this is the position of the Sahibayn and the relied-upon view (mufta bihi).
The text says 'our scholars' indicating the majority Hanafi stance.
2
Understand the qiyas argument
Identify the analogy: just as the estate of a Muslim with no heirs goes to Bayt al-Mal (because the community are his inheritors), similarly the liability of diyah when there is no 'aqila falls on Bayt al-Mal (because the community are his supporters).
The illa is the absence of specific supporters; the community of Muslims stands in.
3
Identify the rare narration
Locate the text's mention of a riwayah shadhdhah from Abu Hanifa holding that the diyah is from the killer's own wealth.
This is a weak transmission, not part of Zahir al-Riwayah.
4
Apply the Hanafi authority hierarchy
Recognise that the rare narration is not followed; the relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is the one from Bayt al-Mal, as indicated by the text's own phrasing ('our scholars' and calling the alternative 'rare').
Zahir al-Riwayah outweighs Nawadir.
5
Compare with other schools
Note the cross-madhhab difference: the Shafi'i school holds that a person without 'aqila must pay from his own wealth. This is a legitimate ikhtilaf (mukhtalaf fih).
The passage does not elaborate on Shafi'i, but the analytical toolbox notes it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال علماؤنا رحمهم الله تعالى : إن القاتل إذا لم يكن له عاقلة والدية في بيت المال إذا كان القاتل مسلماً لأن جماعة المسلمين هم أهل نصرته وليس بعضهم أخص من البعض بذلك، ولهذا إذا مات فميراثه لبيت المال فكذا ما يلزمه من الغرامة يلزم بيت المال . وعن أبي حنيفة رواية شاذة أنها تجب الدية في ماله
Our scholars, may Allah have mercy on them, said: If the killer has no 'aqila, then the blood-money is in the Bayt al-Mal (public treasury) if the killer is Muslim, because the community of Muslims are his supporters, and no one is more specific than others in that. For this reason, if he dies, his inheritance goes to the Bayt al-Mal. Similarly, the liability that obligates him is upon the Bayt al-Mal. And from Abu Hanifa there is a rare narration that the blood-money is obligatory from his own wealth.
Source pages p. 209

Commentary
This passage begins by stating the established Hanafi ruling: if a Muslim killer has no 'aqila (the clan that traditionally pays blood-money), the diyah (blood-money) falls upon the Bayt al-Mal (public treasury). The reasoning is qiyas (analogy): since when a Muslim without heirs dies, his inheritance goes to the Bayt al-Mal because he is from the community of Muslims, similarly, his liabilities are upon the Bayt al-Mal. The passage then mentions a rare narration (riwayah shadhdhah) from Imam Abu Hanifa that the diyah is from the killer's own wealth. This is a weak transmission and not the relied-upon position. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that it is from the Bayt al-Mal. This is from the authority weight of Zahir al-Riwayah.

Key Terms
العاقلة (al-'aqila): The clan or supporters on the father's side who are liable to pay blood-money (diyah) on behalf of a member who commits manslaughter or accidental killing.
بيت المال (Bayt al-Mal): The public treasury of the Islamic state.
رواية شاذة (riwayah shadhdhah): A rare or anomalous transmission, not considered authoritative in the madhhab.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position, where does the diyah come from if the killer has no 'aqila?
A) From the killer's own wealth
B) From the Bayt al-Mal
C) From the victim's family
D) It is not payable

Key Takeaway
When a Muslim killer has no 'aqila, the Hanafi school holds that the diyah is wajib upon the Bayt al-Mal, based on qiyas with inheritance; the rare opposite narration is not relied upon.

The Virtue and Ruling of Bequest (Wasaya)
Explaining the reason for bequest, the hadith on injustice, and best practices

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on bequest and explain what constitutes injustice (al-hayf) according to the hadith.

Core Idea
A bequest is recommended for the wealthy, but injustice—exceeding one-third or bequesting to an heir—is a major sin.

This lesson explores the Hanafi perspective on bequest (wasaya). The passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains the wisdom behind allowing bequests, the Prophetic warning against injustice, and the best practices for testators regarding wealth and priorities.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine the legal classification of bequest. It is sunnah muakkadah for the wealthy, but not recommended for those with little wealth.
2
Locate the dalil
The passage cites the hadith: 'al-hayf fi al-wasiyya min akbar al-kaba'ir'. Recognize this as ahad hadith used as proof.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The word 'al-hayf' is interpreted as exceeding one-third or bequesting to an heir; both are prohibited.
4
Identify the best practices
Priorities: first obligatory payments, then relatives, then neighbors. The testator should bequeath only if he has sufficient wealth.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الوصايا لك سبب الزوال يوصي من إن تذرهم عالة يتكففون الناس ولأن حق الورثة تعلق بماله لانعقاد إليهم وهو استغناؤه عن المال إلا أن الشرع لم يظهر في حق الأجانب بقدر الثلث ليتدارك تقصيره وأظهره في حق الورثة لأن الظاهر أنه لا يتصدق به عليهم تحرزاً عما يتفق لهم من التأذي بالايثار، وقد جاء في الحديث أنه عليه الصلاة والسلام قال «الحيف في الوصية من أكبر الكبائر وفسروه بالزيادة على الثلث وبالوصية للوارث. وقوله «مستحبة الخ» الأفضل لمن كان قليل المال أن لا يوصي بشيء، والأفضل لمن كان له مال كثير أن بما لا معصية فيه. وقدر الأغنياء عند الإمام إذا ترك لكل واحد من الورثة أربعة آلاف دون الوصية، وعن الإمام الفضل عشرة آلاف وفي الموصي الذي أراد أن يوصي ينبغي أن يبدأ بالواجبات فإن لم يكن عليه شيء من الواجبات بدأ بالقرابة فإن كانوا أغنياء فالجيران . وفي الفتاوى : عامل السلطان أوصى بأن يعطي للفقراء كذا كذا من ماله قال أبو القاسم: إن علم بأنه مال غيره لا يحل أخذه وإن علم أنه مختلط بمال غيره جاز أخذه، وإن لم يعلم لا يجوز حتى يتبين أنه ماله قال الفقيه أبو الليث الجواز قول أبي حنيفة لأنه ملكه بالخلط، وعلى قولهما لا يجوز. وفي الخانية : إذا أوصى أن ينفق على فرس فلان جاز وهي وصية لصاحب الفرس .
The Book of Bequests: For you, the reason for [the permissibility of] bequest is lest you leave them as a burden, begging from people. And because the right of the heirs is attached to his wealth due to its connection to them, and he is wealthy and does not need the wealth. However, the Law did not allow [bequest] to non-heirs except up to one-third to make up for his negligence, and it allowed it to heirs because it is apparent that he does not give charity to them, to avoid the harm that comes to them from preferring others. It has come in the hadith that the Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'Injustice in bequest is among the major sins.' And they interpreted it as exceeding one-third and bequest to an heir. His saying: 'It is recommended, etc.' The best for one who has little wealth is not to bequeath anything. The best for one who has much wealth is to bequeath something [as long as there is no disobedience in it]. The measure of wealth for the wealthy according to Imam [Abu Hanifa] is if he leaves four thousand dirhams for each heir without the bequest. And from Imam al-Fadl: ten thousand. And the testator who wishes to bequeath should begin with the obligatory [payments]. If he owes nothing obligatory, he begins with relatives. If they are rich, then neighbors. In al-Fatawa: The agent of the sultan bequeathed that such-and-such be given to the poor from his wealth. Abu al-Qasim said: If it is known that it is another's wealth, taking it is not permissible. And if it is known that it is mixed with another's wealth, taking it is permissible. And if it is not known, it is not permissible until it becomes clear that it is his wealth. Al-Faqih Abu al-Layth [said]: permissibility is the view of Abu Hanifa, because it becomes his property by mixing. And according to the two [Abu Yusuf and Muhammad], it is not permissible. In al-Khaniyya: If he bequeaths that a horse of so-and-so be fed, it is permissible, and it is a bequest for the owner of the horse.
Source pages p. 214

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, a commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq, discusses the rulings and etiquette of bequest (wasaya). It first provides the reason bequest is allowed: to prevent the testator's dependents from becoming destitute and begging, and because the heirs' right to the estate is recognized. However, the Law restricts bequest to non-heirs to one-third, as a concession for the testator's negligence, while bequest to an heir is not allowed because it is assumed the testator does not give charity to them. The hadith of the Prophet (peace be upon him) declares injustice in bequest a major sin, defined as exceeding one-third or bequesting to an heir. The text then gives guidance: for a person with little wealth, it is better not to bequeath; for a wealthy person, it is better to bequeath something without sin. The measure of wealth (for it to be recommended) is if the testator leaves enough for each heir to have four thousand dirhams (according to Imam Abu Hanifa) or ten thousand (according to Imam al-Fadl). The testator should prioritize obligatory payments, then relatives, then neighbors. The passage then cites a fatwa about a government agent who bequeaths from property that may include others' wealth: if it is known to be another's, it is unlawful; if mixed, it is permissible according to Abu Hanifa (because mixing establishes ownership), but not according to the two companions. Finally, a ruling from al-Khaniyya: bequeathing to feed a particular horse is valid and constitutes a bequest to the horse's owner.

Key Terms
الحيف (al-hayf): Injustice in bequest: exceeding one-third or bequest to an heir.
الوصية (al-wasiyya): A bequest or testament, transfer of property after death up to one-third for non-heirs.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the hadith mentioned in the passage, what constitutes 'injustice in bequest' (الحيف في الوصية)?
A) Bequesting to a non-relative
B) Exceeding one-third of the estate or bequesting to an heir
C) Bequesting less than one-third
D) Not making a bequest at all

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, bequest is a recommended act for the wealthy, but it must not exceed one-third or be to an heir, as that constitutes injustice (al-hayf) which is a major sin.

The Measure of Wealth for Bequest and Intra-Hanafi Ikhtilaf
Explaining the reason for bequest, the hadith on injustice, and best practices

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two different measures of wealth for recommending bequest within the Hanafi school and explain the practical consequence of the disagreement.

Core Idea
The Hanafi school has an internal disagreement: bequest is recommended if the testator leaves each heir 4000 dirhams (Abu Hanifa) or 10,000 (Imam al-Fadl).

This lesson focuses on a specific internal Hanafi ikhtilaf regarding the threshold of wealth that makes bequest recommended. Imam Abu Hanifa set the measure at leaving each heir 4000 dirhams, while Imam al-Fadl set it at 10,000. Understanding this dispute helps in applying the ruling correctly.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Bequest is sunnah for the wealthy. But what counts as 'wealthy'? The passage cites two opinions.
2
Identify the two authorities
Imam Abu Hanifa: the testator leaves each heir 4000 dirhams. Imam al-Fadl: 10,000 dirhams.
3
Analyse the basis of difference
The text gives no explicit reasoning. The difference likely stems from different assessments of sufficiency or local economic conditions.
4
Determine the mufta bihi
The passage does not specify which is relied upon; both views are presented as valid transmissions.

Source Text

Bequest from Mixed Property: Hanafi Ruling and Intra-School Dispute
Explaining the reason for bequest, the hadith on injustice, and best practices

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on bequest from property that may include others' wealth and distinguish between the views of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
Bequeathing from mixed property is valid according to Abu Hanifa (ownership by mixing) but invalid according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad unless the property is clearly the testator's.

This lesson examines a specific fatwa from the passage regarding a government agent (عامل السلطان) who bequeaths from wealth that may be mixed with other people's property. The Hanafi school has an internal disagreement on whether such a bequest is valid, based on the concept of ownership through mixing (ملكه بالخلط).

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
The issue is about the validity of bequest from property that is mixed with others' wealth.
2
Identify the two views
Abu Hanifa: permissible because mixing establishes ownership. Sahibayn: not permissible unless it is clearly his own.
3
Analyse the basis of difference
Abu Hanifa relies on the principle that mixing transforms ownership; Sahibayn require clarity and certainty.
4
Determine the mufta bihi
The text mentions al-Faqih Abu al-Layth supports Abu Hanifa's view ('الجواز قول أبي حنيفة'), implying that Abu Hanifa's view is preferred, but the mufta bihi is not explicitly stated.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الوصايا لك سبب الزوال يوصي من إن تذرهم عالة يتكففون الناس ولأن حق الورثة تعلق بماله لانعقاد إليهم وهو استغناؤه عن المال إلا أن الشرع لم يظهر في حق الأجانب بقدر الثلث ليتدارك تقصيره وأظهره في حق الورثة لأن الظاهر أنه لا يتصدق به عليهم تحرزاً عما يتفق لهم من التأذي بالايثار، وقد جاء في الحديث أنه عليه الصلاة والسلام قال «الحيف في الوصية من أكبر الكبائر وفسروه بالزيادة على الثلث وبالوصية للوارث. وقوله «مستحبة الخ» الأفضل لمن كان قليل المال أن لا يوصي بشيء، والأفضل لمن كان له مال كثير أن بما لا معصية فيه. وقدر الأغنياء عند الإمام إذا ترك لكل واحد من الورثة أربعة آلاف دون الوصية، وعن الإمام الفضل عشرة آلاف وفي الموصي الذي أراد أن يوصي ينبغي أن يبدأ بالواجبات فإن لم يكن عليه شيء من الواجبات بدأ بالقرابة فإن كانوا أغنياء فالجيران . وفي الفتاوى : عامل السلطان أوصى بأن يعطي للفقراء كذا كذا من ماله قال أبو القاسم: إن علم بأنه مال غيره لا يحل أخذه وإن علم أنه مختلط بمال غيره جاز أخذه، وإن لم يعلم لا يجوز حتى يتبين أنه ماله قال الفقيه أبو الليث الجواز قول أبي حنيفة لأنه ملكه بالخلط، وعلى قولهما لا يجوز. وفي الخانية : إذا أوصى أن ينفق على فرس فلان جاز وهي وصية لصاحب الفرس .
The Book of Bequests: For you, the reason for [the permissibility of] bequest is lest you leave them as a burden, begging from people. And because the right of the heirs is attached to his wealth due to its connection to them, and he is wealthy and does not need the wealth. However, the Law did not allow [bequest] to non-heirs except up to one-third to make up for his negligence, and it allowed it to heirs because it is apparent that he does not give charity to them, to avoid the harm that comes to them from preferring others. It has come in the hadith that the Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'Injustice in bequest is among the major sins.' And they interpreted it as exceeding one-third and bequest to an heir. His saying: 'It is recommended, etc.' The best for one who has little wealth is not to bequeath anything. The best for one who has much wealth is to bequeath something [as long as there is no disobedience in it]. The measure of wealth for the wealthy according to Imam [Abu Hanifa] is if he leaves four thousand dirhams for each heir without the bequest. And from Imam al-Fadl: ten thousand. And the testator who wishes to bequeath should begin with the obligatory [payments]. If he owes nothing obligatory, he begins with relatives. If they are rich, then neighbors. In al-Fatawa: The agent of the sultan bequeathed that such-and-such be given to the poor from his wealth. Abu al-Qasim said: If it is known that it is another's wealth, taking it is not permissible. And if it is known that it is mixed with another's wealth, taking it is permissible. And if it is not known, it is not permissible until it becomes clear that it is his wealth. Al-Faqih Abu al-Layth [said]: permissibility is the view of Abu Hanifa, because it becomes his property by mixing. And according to the two [Abu Yusuf and Muhammad], it is not permissible. In al-Khaniyya: If he bequeaths that a horse of so-and-so be fed, it is permissible, and it is a bequest for the owner of the horse.
Source pages p. 214

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, a commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq, discusses the rulings and etiquette of bequest (wasaya). It first provides the reason bequest is allowed: to prevent the testator's dependents from becoming destitute and begging, and because the heirs' right to the estate is recognized. However, the Law restricts bequest to non-heirs to one-third, as a concession for the testator's negligence, while bequest to an heir is not allowed because it is assumed the testator does not give charity to them. The hadith of the Prophet (peace be upon him) declares injustice in bequest a major sin, defined as exceeding one-third or bequesting to an heir. The text then gives guidance: for a person with little wealth, it is better not to bequeath; for a wealthy person, it is better to bequeath something without sin. The measure of wealth (for it to be recommended) is if the testator leaves enough for each heir to have four thousand dirhams (according to Imam Abu Hanifa) or ten thousand (according to Imam al-Fadl). The testator should prioritize obligatory payments, then relatives, then neighbors. The passage then cites a fatwa about a government agent who bequeaths from property that may include others' wealth: if it is known to be another's, it is unlawful; if mixed, it is permissible according to Abu Hanifa (because mixing establishes ownership), but not according to the two companions. Finally, a ruling from al-Khaniyya: bequeathing to feed a particular horse is valid and constitutes a bequest to the horse's owner.

Key Terms
الحيف (al-hayf): Injustice in bequest: exceeding one-third or bequest to an heir.
الوصية (al-wasiyya): A bequest or testament, transfer of property after death up to one-third for non-heirs.
الخلط (al-khalt): Mixing of property; in this context, mixing establishes ownership according to Abu Hanifa.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the fatwa in the passage, what is the ruling of bequest from property that is known to be mixed with another's wealth?
A) Always permissible regardless of knowledge.
B) Permissible according to Abu Hanifa, but not according to Sahibayn.
C) Never permissible.
D) Permissible only if the testator is the ruler.

Key Takeaway
Bequest from mixed property is valid according to Abu Hanifa (ownership by mixing) but invalid according to Sahibayn unless the property is clearly the testator's.

Validity vs. Enforceability: Bequest Exceeding One-Third
Commentary on matn ruling: bequest exceeding one-third not valid

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between sihha (validity) and jawaz (permissibility) in Hanafi wasiyya law and state the ruling for a bequest exceeding one-third.

Core Idea
A bequest exceeding one-third is valid but not enforceable without heirs' consent.

The commentator of Kanz al-Daqa'iq refines the wording of a foundational ruling. He argues that saying 'لا تصح' (it is not valid) is more precise than 'لا تجوز' (it is not permissible) because invalidity implies impermissibility, but impermissibility does not imply invalidity. This distinction matters: a bequest over one-third is legally sound but cannot be executed unless the heirs agree. The passage also explains how to interpret such a bequest as two separate parts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling and Its Wording
Read the matn quote: 'ولا تصح بما زاد على الثلث'. The author states a bequest exceeding one-third is not valid (sahih). Contrast this with the Hidayah's 'ولا تجوز' (not permissible).
The commentator elevates precision in legal language.
2
Analyze the Distinction Between Sihha and Jawaz
The commentator explains that lack of sihha (validity) always entails lack of jawaz (permissibility). But lack of jawaz does not necessarily mean lack of sihha. Example: a sale during Friday prayer is valid but impermissible. Here, a bequest exceeding one-third is valid (sahih) in itself but not enforceable (nafidh) without consent.
Sihha relates to the essential elements; jawaz relates to enforceability.
3
Apply the Ruling to a Concrete Scenario
Consider a testator who bequeaths 50% of his estate to a non-heir. The bequest is sahih (valid) regarding the first third; it is batil (void) regarding the excess unless heirs consent. The bequest is treated as two separate bequests: one for the valid third, one for the conditional excess.
Heirs' consent makes the excess enforceable.
4
Resolve the Apparent Linguistic Difficulty
The word 'لا تصح' negates validity only for the excess portion, not the entire bequest. The commentator resolves this by treating the bequest as multiple: the testator's statement is interpreted as bequeathing only one-third, with the excess contingent on condition (heirs' consent).
This linguistic interpretation avoids total nullification of the bequest.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : (ولا تصح بما زاد على الثلث فهذه العبارة أولى من عبارة الهداية حيث قال : ولا تجوز لأنه يلزم من عدم الصحة عدم الجواز ولا يلزم من عدم الجواز عدم الصحة حتى ينفذ بل يتوقف على الإجازة كما سيأتي إن شاء الله تعالى. قال بعض المتأخرين : يعني لا يجوز بما زاد على الثلث حتى لا يجوز في حق الفاضل على الثلث بل في حق الثلث، فقط لا أنه لا تجوز هذه الوصية أصلاً. فإن قلت: كيف جاز استعمال اللفظ في بعض مدلولاته دون بعض وبأي وجه أمكن ذلك؟ قلت: يجعله في حكم وصايا متعددة بأن يجعل قوله أوصيت لفلان بثلثي مالي في قوة قوله أوصيت له بثلثه دون الزائد والوصية تارة تكون منجزة وتارة معلقة بشرط فيجب أن يعلم بأن تعلق الوصية بالشرط جائز.
He (the author) said: 'And it is not valid if it exceeds one-third.' So this expression is better than the expression of al-Hidayah, where he said: 'And it is not permissible,' because from lack of validity (sihha) it necessarily follows lack of permissibility (jawaz), but from lack of permissibility it does not necessarily follow lack of validity. Therefore, it is not valid (i.e., not binding) but rather depends on consent, as will come. Some later scholars said: It does not permit what exceeds one-third; i.e., it is not allowed in regard to the excess over one-third, but it is allowed in regard to the one-third itself; it does not mean that this bequest is not permissible at all. If you say: How can the word be used in some of its meanings and not others? And how is that possible? I say: It is treated as multiple bequests; i.e., his saying 'I bequeath to so-and-so two-thirds of my wealth' is considered as saying 'I bequeath to him one-third' without the excess. And bequest sometimes is immediate and sometimes conditional upon a condition, so it must be known that attaching a bequest to a condition is permissible.
Source pages p. 214

Commentary
This section addresses the precise wording of the ruling in Kanz al-Daqa'iq: 'ولا تصح بما زاد على الثلث' (it is not valid if it exceeds one-third). The commentator argues this wording is superior to the Hidayah's 'ولا تجوز' (it is not permissible) because 'sihha' (validity) is a narrower concept: if something is invalid, it is also not permissible, but it could be impermissible yet still valid (e.g., a sale during Friday prayer). Here, a bequest exceeding one-third is not valid (i.e., not binding) unless the heirs give consent. The commentator then explains that the word 'لا تصح' can be used to negate validity only for the excess portion, not for the entire bequest. He resolves this by treating the bequest as if it were two separate bequests: one for the first third (valid) and one for the excess (invalid). He also notes that bequests can be conditional, and such conditions are valid.

Key Terms
الصحة (al-sihha): Legal validity; here, the bequest is valid but not binding (nāfidh) without consent.
الجواز (al-jawāz): Permissibility; something is ja'iz if it is legally allowed. Here, the commentator distinguishes it from validity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the commentary, why does the author of Kanz al-Daqa'iq prefer the wording 'لا تصح' over 'لا تجوز'?
A) Because 'لا تصح' is more emphatic.
B) Because invalidity implies impermissibility, but impermissibility does not imply invalidity.
C) Because 'لا تجوز' is not found in classical fiqh.
D) Because the Hidayah's ruling was incorrect.

Key Takeaway
A bequest exceeding one-third is sahih (valid) in its essential form but its enforcement is suspended until the heirs consent; the wording 'لا تصح' captures this nuance better than 'لا تجوز'.
